            interstitial: googletag.sizeMapping()
                .addSize([0, 0], [640, 480])
                .build()
        };

        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/advs.202304067')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="edc3c9ad1c164163a1dad17f5a4fb5d1"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="edc3c9ad1c164163a1dad17f5a4fb5d1">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '75e6d4d3-05d4-41bf-94b7-5f708d8882e7'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d19439713" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>High-capacity O3-type layered sodium oxides are considered one of the most promising cathode materials for the next generation of Na-ion batteries (NIBs). However, these cathodes usually suffer from low high-rate capacity and poor cycling stability due to structure deformation, native air sensitivity, and interfacial side reactions. Herein, a multi-site substituted strategy is employed to enhance the stability of O3-type NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. Simulations indicate that the Ti substitution decreases the charge density of Ni ions and improves the antioxidative capability of the material. In addition, the synergistic effect of K<sup>+</sup> and Ti<sup>4+</sup> significantly reduces the formation energy of Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy and delivers an ultra-low lattice strain during the repeated Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion. In situ characterizations verify that the complicated phase transformation is mitigated during the charge/discharge process, resulting in greatly improved structure stability. The co-substituted cathode delivers a high-rate capacity of 97&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 5&nbsp;C and excellent capacity retention of 81% after 400 cycles at 0.5&nbsp;C. The full cell paired with commercial hard carbon anode also exhibits high capacity and long cycling life. This dual-ion substitution strategy will provide a universal approach for the new rational design of high-capacity cathode materials for NIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs6431-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs6431-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Na-ion batteries (NIBs) have been considered as the potential alternative to Li-ion batteries (LIBs) in large-scale energy storage devices because of abundant reserves and low cost.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0001_R_d19439662e651" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Rare Met. ACS Energy Lett. Chem. Rev.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similar to LIBs, cathode materials are crucial to the operating voltage, energy density, and even cycle life of NIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0002_R_d19439662e656" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nano Energy Interdisciplinary Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The layered transition metal (TM) oxides Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>TMO<sub>2</sub> have been widely studied as cathodes for NIBs due to their obvious advantages including a simple synthesis process and attractive electrochemical properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0003_R_d19439662e667" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. eScience">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> P2 phase (<i>x</i> &lt; 0.8, Na<sup>+</sup> occupies trigonal prismatic sites, O sheets stack in an ABBA sequence) and O3 phase (0.8 ≤ <i>x</i> ≤ 1, Na<sup>+</sup> occupies octahedral sites, O sheets stack in an ABCABC sequence) are representative cathode structures, and the structure can be predicted by modulating the Na content and cation potential <i>Φ</i>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0004_R_d19439662e683" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Science Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With higher reversible capacity relative to the Na-poor P2-cathodes in the same voltage window, the Na-rich O3-cathodes have been studied as the candidate cathode materials matched with sodium-free anodes to assemble full cells.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0005_R_d19439662e688" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Funct. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Based on Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup>/Ni<sup>4+</sup> redox, the typical O3-type cathode NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> delivers a large reversible capacity and high average discharge voltage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0001_R_d19439662e708" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Rare Met. ACS Energy Lett. Chem. Rev.">1</a>, <a href="#advs6431-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0006_R_d19439662e708" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Inorg. Chem. Adv. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, their large-scale application is suffering from some severe challenges. For example, the detrimental multiphase transformations and unavoidable electrolyte erosion during Na<sup>+</sup> de/intercalation process are prone to rapid capacity decay and deteriorated rate capability of NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0007_R_d19439662e722" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further, most O3-type cathodes are sensitive to moisture and susceptible to Na<sup>+</sup> release via Na<sup>+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup> exchange or TM oxidation, thus inducing severe degradation of the electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0008_R_d19439662e734" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. Mater. J. Electrochem. Soc. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Appl. Surf. Sci.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To address these issues, the conventional strategy is to adopt surface coating and elemental substitution.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0009_R_d19439662e741" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - eScience">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Usually, the surface coating has been considered a direct method to protect the cathode from electrolyte erosion; however, it cannot modulate the unfavorable phase transition of the bulk phase. Chemical substitution has been demonstrated to improve electrochemical performance by optimizing the bulk structure and surface interface properties of the cathode material. For example, introducing small amounts of Mg<sup>2+</sup> into the TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs can effectively suppress irreversible multiphase transitions to achieve high structural stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0010_R_d19439662e750" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Mater. Chem. A Mater. Chem. Front.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It has proven to be essential to investigate the mechanism of multi-site doping to synergistically optimize the electrochemical performance of cathode, such as Cu/Ti co-substituted NaNi<sub>0.45</sub>Cu<sub>0.05</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0011_R_d19439662e767" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fe/Mg co-substituted NaNi<sub>0.35</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub> O<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0012_R_d19439662e783" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Mater. Chem. Front.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Co/Ti co-doped NaNi<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.25</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Co<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0013_R_d19439662e799" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc. The above-doped transition metal ions are generally introduced into the TM layer (a small amount of Mg<sup>2+</sup> may occupy the Na layer) to suppress the unfavorable multiphase transition. Some pioneers have confirmed that reasonable Na site substitution is also important to enhance structural stability and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics. In this case, an optimal dual-site substitution combining the advantages of both Na layer and TM layer substitutions may obtain excellent electrochemical properties.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, K<sup>+</sup> and Ti<sup>4+</sup> have attracted our attention and tend to partially replace Na<sup>+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup> according to their ionic radius and elemental valence states. First, it is demonstrated that Ti<sup>4+</sup> can efficiently expand the solid solution reaction region, improve air stability, and inhibit electrolyte erosion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0011_R_d19439662e822" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">11</a>, <a href="#advs6431-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0014_R_d19439662e822" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Mater. Today Commun.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the introduction of K<sup>+</sup> facilitates (de) sodiation and stabilizes the structure due to the larger ionic radius and lower electronegativity than Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0015_R_d19439662e831" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Electrochim. Acta ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Nat. Commun. Electrochim. Acta">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, taking full advantage of both elements offers the possibility of designing high-specific energy and long-life cathode materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs6431-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs6431-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs6431-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs6431-sec-0030-title">2.1 Microstructure and Composition Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs6431-fig-0001">1a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), the NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> sample (referred to as NaNMO) delivers a plate-like morphology with an average particle size of ≈400&nbsp;nm and thickness of ≈200&nbsp;nm. However, though the synthesis method and sintering procedure are identical to those of NaNMO, the particle size of the Na<sub>0.99</sub>K<sub>0.01</sub>Ni<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> sample (referred to as NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O) increases significantly to ≈1&nbsp;µm, which is mainly due to that Ti can facilitate the crystal growth during sintering (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6431-fig-0001">1b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0016_R_d19439662e890" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The formation of large-sized single crystals and the reduced specific surface area tends to cause a decreased contact area with air, thus inhibiting the production of residual bases on the surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0011_R_d19439662e895" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The interplanar distances between adjacent lattice stripes in the HRTEM pattern is 2.19&nbsp;Å, in accordance with the <i>d</i>-spacing value in the (104) plane calculated from the Bragg equation (2<i>d</i> sin<i>θ</i> = n<i>λ</i>) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6431-fig-0001">1d</a>). EDS shows that all elements are uniformly distributed on the sample surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6431-fig-0001">1e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The XRD patterns of the four samples are considered as O3-type <i>α</i>-NaFeO<sub>2</sub> layered structures with rhombohedral <i>R-3m</i> space group (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0011_R_d19439662e928" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">11</a>, <a href="#advs6431-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0017_R_d19439662e928" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The observed and calculated patterns of NaNMO and NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O are in excellent agreement (<i>R</i><sub>p</sub> &lt; 1.95%, <i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> &lt; 3.28%) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6431-fig-0001">1f</a>), and the detailed lattice parameters calculated by the Rietveld method are summarized in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The apparent changes in lattice parameters indicate that K and Ti have been successfully doped into the host structure of NaNMO. According to previous studies, K<sup>+</sup> and Ti<sup>4+</sup> are most likely to occupy Na sites and Mn sites in the Na-based layered oxide structure, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0011_R_d19439662e958" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">11</a>, <a href="#advs6431-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0018_R_d19439662e958" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Res.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6431-fig-0001">1g</a> shows the lattice structures of NaNMO and NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O constructed based on the refined lattice parameters. The difference between the layer spacing of NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O (<i>d</i><sub>Na</sub> = 3.2020 Å, <i>d</i><sub>TM</sub> = 2.1513 Å) and NaNMO (<i>d</i><sub>Na</sub> = 3.1921 Å, <i>d</i><sub>TM</sub> = 2.1327 Å) can be attributed to the larger ionic radii of K<sup>+</sup> (1.38 Å) and Ti<sup>4+</sup> (0.60 Å) than those of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02 Å) and Mn<sup>4+</sup> (0.53 Å), respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0015_R_d19439662e997" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Electrochim. Acta ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Nat. Commun. Electrochim. Acta">15</a>, <a href="#advs6431-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0017_R_d19439662e997" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs6431-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/29d3827d-f5d0-4e5c-a4f0-a58baf03d735/advs6431-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/29d3827d-f5d0-4e5c-a4f0-a58baf03d735/advs6431-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/64e97814-2cb7-4eda-95f6-03106420820a/advs6431-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/29d3827d-f5d0-4e5c-a4f0-a58baf03d735/advs6431-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs6431-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202304067" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM images of a) NaNMO and b) NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O. HR-TEM images with FFT patterns as an insert of c) NaNMO and d) NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O. e) EDS mappings of NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O. f) XRD patterns and Rietveld refinement plots of NaNMO and NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O. g) Schematic diagrams of crystal structure model of NaNMO and NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NM Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs6431-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs6431-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performances</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical properties of all samples were investigated in Na<sup>+</sup> half cell with a window of 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V. NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMO delivers a higher reversible capacity (130 vs 125 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) than NaNMO (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs6431-fig-0002">2a</a>), which is mainly due to that the large size of K<sup>+</sup> pinned in the Na layer can reduce the strength of the Na‒O bond and the formation energy of the Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0015_R_d19439662e1068" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Electrochim. Acta ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Nat. Commun. Electrochim. Acta">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although the introduction of inactive tetravalent Ti<sup>4+</sup> (Ti<sup>4+</sup>: <i>t</i><sub>2g</sub><sup>0</sup><i>e</i><sub>g</sub><sup>0</sup>:<i>d</i><sup>0</sup>) will inevitably reduce the initial discharge capacity, the irreversible phase transition and Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering above 3&nbsp;V are significantly suppressed due to the solid-solution reaction of a large region.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0019_R_d19439662e1091" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Storage Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6431-fig-0002">2b</a>, four redox peaks (2.81/2.49, 3.28/3.18, 3.55/3.46, and 3.70/3.52&nbsp;V) are observed in the CV of NaNMO, corresponding exactly to the four voltage plateaus in the charge/discharge curves. These four redox peaks are related to Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup>/Ni<sup>4+</sup> redox reaction, Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering, and O3-O3′-P3-P3′-P3″ multiple phase transition. During the cycle, the positions of the four pairs of redox peaks shifted significantly, indicating the poor reversibility of the multiphase transitions. When K<sup>+</sup> is introduced into NaNMO, the intensity of the redox peak in the low-voltage region (2.80/2.49&nbsp;V) increases significantly, which demonstrates that K<sup>+</sup> can promote Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion during charging/discharging at lower voltages. With the introduction of Ti<sup>4+</sup>, the redox peaks of the CV curves above 3&nbsp;V are significantly suppressed, and more symmetrical anodic/cathodic peaks at higher voltages (2.87/2.66&nbsp;V) can be observed. Since the bond energy of Ti‒O bonds (666.5 KJ&nbsp;mol<sup>−1</sup>) is significantly higher than the Mn‒O (362 KJ&nbsp;mol<sup>−1</sup>) and Ni‒O bonds (391.6 KJ&nbsp;mol<sup>−1</sup>), sharing oxygen with Ti can stabilize the crystal structure and inhibit the multiphase transition due to the slip of the transition metal layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0020_R_d19439662e1123" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Power Sources">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the smaller difference in ionic radii between Mn<sup>4+</sup> and Ti<sup>4+</sup> is favorable for inhibiting the transition metal layer ordering and multiphase transition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0021_R_d19439662e1133" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nat. Commun.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs6431-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/86e0a2b8-1dfd-479c-8d16-5d9efab26bea/advs6431-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/86e0a2b8-1dfd-479c-8d16-5d9efab26bea/advs6431-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/98611d6a-909b-41d1-a30a-62368d31f799/advs6431-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/86e0a2b8-1dfd-479c-8d16-5d9efab26bea/advs6431-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs6431-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202304067" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Galvanostatic charge/discharge curves (GCDs) of four materials in the first cycle. GCDs were obtained at 0.1 C. b) Cyclic voltammograms curves (CVs) of four materials in the first three cycles. c) GCDs of NaNMO and NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O cycled at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, and 5.0 C. d) Cycling performance of NaNMO and NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O at 0.5 C in the voltage range of 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V. e) Schematic diagram showing the discharge process and f) Cycling performance of NaNMO//HC and NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O//HC full cells between 1.5 and 4.0&nbsp;V during 500 cycles at 1 C. g) Nyquist plots of EIS and corresponding fitting curves of NaNMO and NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O before and after cycling.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The discharge capacity of NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O is much higher than that of NaNMO (97.1 vs 75.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) at 5 C, which accounts for 73.6% and 59.4% of their initial capacity, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6431-fig-0002">2c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the energy density of NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O is superior to NaNMO at various current densities due to the higher average voltage, e.g. 404.5&nbsp;versus 375.1&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). Although the initial discharge capacities of all samples exceed 120 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C, NaNMO experienced the fastest capacity decay, dropping to 37 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 400 cycles, which corresponds to 33% of the initial capacity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6431-fig-0002">2d</a>). With the introduction of a single element (K or Ti), the capacity retention of NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMO increases to 41%, while it reaches 62% for NaNMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). More importantly, the simultaneous introduction of both elements minimizes the initial capacity loss, and further shows a high discharge capacity of 128 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and excellent electrochemical reversibility for 1000 cycles (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). Based on the electrochemical properties of the different doping contents demonstrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, the optimized K and Ti contents are determined as 0.01 and 0.1, respectively.</p>
            
            <p>In addition to the above remarkable electrochemical performances in sodium half cell, NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O also exhibits excellent full cell performances, which initially demonstrates the practical commercial viability of the modified cathode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6431-fig-0002">2e</a>). The anode is pre-cycled three times before assembling the full cell due to the low coulombic efficiency of hard carbon (HC) in the first cycle.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0022_R_d19439662e1239" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Nano">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information) show the rate performance and GCDs profiles of the HC anode, respectively. NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O delivers a high reversible capacity of 95.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a high average working voltage of ≈3.24&nbsp;V, and can successfully illuminate commercial light-emitting diodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6431-fig-0002">2f</a>). Furthermore, the full cell with NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O cathode shows better cyclic stability (retained capacity of 73% vs 56% after 500 cycles, at 1 C) than the reference NaNMO full cell. The value of the Warburg impedance <i>Z</i><sub>w</sub> depends on the interfacial impedance <i>R</i><sub>f</sub>, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion impedance in the electrolyte <i>R</i><sub>s</sub>, and the charge transfer impedance <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>, etc.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0023_R_d19439662e1280" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were carried out to further assess the resistance of NaNMO and NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O before and after cycling, and the detailed resistance data are shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). The samples cycled for 50 cycles are labeled as C-NaNMO and C-NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O, respectively. Since residual alkaline substances and by-products on the particle surface may severely impair the reaction kinetics of the cathode, the <i>R</i><sub>f</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of NaNMO and C-NaNMO are significantly increased compared to the optimized materials, which confirms the enhanced electrode reaction kinetics for NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O owing to its stronger structure and more stable interface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6431-fig-0002">2g</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0008_R_d19439662e1311" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. Mater. J. Electrochem. Soc. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Appl. Surf. Sci.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs6431-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs6431-sec-0050-title">2.3 Structural Evolution</h3>
            
            <p>In situ XRD experiments were carried out to investigate the effect of K/Ti co-doping on structural evolution during Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion. As a classical O3-type cathode, NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> undergoes multiple phase transitions of O3-O3′-P3-P3′-P3″ during the cycle, which is consistent with the reported literature (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0006_R_d19439662e1337" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Inorg. Chem. Adv. Mater.">6</a>, <a href="#advs6431-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0024_R_d19439662e1337" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Ceram. Int. J. Mater. Chem. A">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O, upon initial charging, the (003), (006), (101), and (012) peaks of the O3 phase split into two whereas the intensity of the (104)<sub>O3</sub> peak clearly decreases, revealing the formation of hexagonal P3 phase via the O3-P3 biphasic reaction, corresponding to the plateau region at ≈2.8&nbsp;V in GCD curve (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs6431-fig-0003">3a</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0025_R_d19439662e1355" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Note that with the further extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>, the (104)<sub>O3</sub> peak disappears, accompanied by the increased intensity of the (105)<sub>P3</sub> peak, implying the complete disappearance of the O3 phase and the generation of a pure P3 phase. No new peaks appeared until charging to 4.0&nbsp;V, indicating that the doping of K/Ti promotes the solid solution reaction (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0026_R_d19439662e1373" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Mater. Chem. A">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It has been demonstrated that the phase transition is closely related to the local structure of the Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0027_R_d19439662e1381" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As Na<sup>+</sup> is extracted from the lattice, the formation energy of Na<sup>+</sup> in the prismatic sites is reduced, further causing the formation of the P3 phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0017_R_d19439662e1390" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the discharge, the XRD pattern undergoes an opposite evolution, demonstrating that high-reversible structural evolution occurred during cycling. This result indicates the strong K‒O bond and Ti‒O bond can effectively mitigate the multiple phase transitions detrimental to long-term stability. The calculated lattice parameters of NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O at different charging/discharging states are further extracted from the in situ XRD. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6431-fig-0003">3b</a> shows that the deviations of <i>a</i>, <i>c</i>, and <i>V</i> are 3.17%, 6.05%, and 0.59%, respectively. The smaller volume strain stabilizes the crystalline structure of the cathode, preventing particle damage and producing less inactive insulating material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0028_R_d19439662e1410" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Nano Energy">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6431-fig-0003">3d</a> briefly illustrates the structural evolution of NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O during the intercalation/ deintercalation process.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs6431-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/83a72656-a187-4413-986e-db3507a6cec2/advs6431-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/83a72656-a187-4413-986e-db3507a6cec2/advs6431-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3600a171-be05-495b-9633-a8503206f205/advs6431-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/83a72656-a187-4413-986e-db3507a6cec2/advs6431-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs6431-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202304067" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) In situ XRD patterns of NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O during the charge–discharge cycle. b) Calculated lattice parameters (<i>a</i>, <i>c</i>, and <i>V</i>) of NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O at different voltages. c) Corresponding voltage profile. d) Schematic illustration showing the crystal structural evolution of NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O upon Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion. Ex situ Raman patterns of e) NaNMO and f) NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Raman is an effective tool for revealing structural changes in electrodes and is widely used in the electrochemical analysis of battery systems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0029_R_d19439662e1471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6431-fig-0003">3e,f</a>, the Raman peak centered at 475 and 585 cm<sup>−1</sup>, one of the typical features of layered cathode materials, represents the <i>E</i><sub>1g</sub> (O‒M‒O bending) and <i>A</i><sub>1g</sub> (M‒O stretching) modes, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0030_R_d19439662e1488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Joule ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the Raman peak located at 382 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponds to the <i>E</i><sub>2g</sub> of Na‒O vibrations.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0031_R_d19439662e1499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Rev.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> More importantly, the Raman peak of C-NaNMO shifts to a lower wave number, which is possibly due to the lattice extension on the <i>c</i>-axis and the change in the interatomic distance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0032_R_d19439662e1506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Phys. Chem. C">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the Raman characteristic peak of C-NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O remains in its original position, implying excellent structural reversibility and further accounting for the remarkable cycling stability.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs6431-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs6431-sec-0060-title">2.4 Interface Composition and Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>The electrode sheets after cycling were tested to further investigate the modification mechanism. The higher monoclinic phase peak in the XRD pattern of C-NaNMO than that of C-NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub> indicates the poor reversibility of structural evolution in C-NaNMO (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs6431-fig-0004">4a</a>). The cathode-electrolyte interphase (CEI) is a significant constraint to improving the electrochemical performance of the cathode. We performed SEM and XPS tests to observe the CEI evolution of NaNMO and NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O during cycling. The CEI layer generally consists of various products from the oxidative decomposition of electrolytes, dissolution of transition metals, and other side reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0014_R_d19439662e1540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Mater. Today Commun.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6431-fig-0004">4b,c</a>, the particle surface and the interstices between the primary particles of C-NaNMO are covered with a large amount of by-products, which is due to the reconstruction of the surface crystal structure caused by the dissolution of the transition metal from the crystal lattice.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0014_R_d19439662e1548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Mater. Today Commun.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, for C-NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O, the surface remains relatively smooth and the morphology remains well maintained, indicating that the interface side reactions were effectively depressed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0033_R_d19439662e1558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Power Sources J. Phys. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The XPS spectra of both electrodes show typical peaks associated with species such as PVDF, NaF, C‒O, C═O, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, active material (A.M.), and amorphous carbon black. It has been demonstrated that the concentration of carbonate deposited on the cathode surface is proportional to the decomposition rate by the electrolyte consisting of carbonate functional groups.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0034_R_d19439662e1568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Mater. Chem. Phys.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, the low surface carbonate peaks of NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O imply excellent interfacial stability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6431-fig-0004">4d,f</a>). It can be observed that the CEI layer of NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O has a higher NaF content, which plays a major role in the overall interface stability. NaF-rich CEI layer can facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and alleviate electrode cracking, due to the low Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barrier (0.12&nbsp;eV) and high shear modulus (31.4&nbsp;GPa) of NaF (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6431-fig-0004">4e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0035_R_d19439662e1596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The result demonstrates that the reduced surface oxygen peak and the larger C/O ratio of C-NaNMO imply a thicker CEI layer formed on the cathode surface (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0036_R_d19439662e1604" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Chem Mater">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In short, proper K/Ti doping can induce the formation of a NaF-rich, thin but stable CEI layer, thus conferring better cycling performance to NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs6431-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a3c7f928-9b54-411f-8efe-6ab43be077fc/advs6431-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a3c7f928-9b54-411f-8efe-6ab43be077fc/advs6431-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5448f482-9471-4929-b8eb-e000545f1132/advs6431-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a3c7f928-9b54-411f-8efe-6ab43be077fc/advs6431-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs6431-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202304067" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD diffraction patterns of NaNMO, NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O, C-NaNMO, and C-NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O. SEM images of b) C-NaNMO and c) C-NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O. Comparison of the d) C 1<i>s</i>, e) F 1<i>s</i>, and f) O 1<i>s</i> XPS spectra of C-NaNMO and C-NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs6431-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs6431-sec-0070-title">2.5 Theoretical Calculation of K/Ti Co-Doped Oxide Compounds</h3>
            
            <p>To further understand the origin of improved air and structural stability of NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O, DFT calculations were carried out to investigate the effect of K/Ti co-doping on the intrinsic electronic structure and Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy formation energy. The charge density difference map between the TM layer and the Na layer of NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O structure is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs6431-fig-0005">5a</a>. The introduction of Ti<sup>4+</sup> can effectively increase the number of electrons around oxygen and decrease the number of electrons around TM via promoting electronic delocalization (yellow represents the region of electron accumulation, and blue represents the region of electron reduction). The electron-deficient state of the TM<sup>n+</sup> can inhibit the spontaneous oxidation reactions and the release of lattice Na upon exposure to air.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0023_R_d19439662e1688" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To understand the redox mechanism theoretically, DFT calculations were performed to obtain the Density of States (DOS) for the four materials. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6431-fig-0005">5b,c</a>, and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a> (Supporting Information), Ni 3<i>d</i> and O 2<i>p</i> orbitals dominate the electronic states near the Fermi level, suggesting that the electron transfer can be compensated by the redox reaction of Ni ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0028_R_d19439662e1704" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Nano Energy">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the conduction band energy levels of the TM 3<i>d</i> orbitals and O 2<i>p</i> orbitals of NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O are clearly shifted to higher energy levels, which implies that the energy level of the doped material is higher when gaining electrons during the discharge process (conduction band energy levels get electrons), that is, the discharge voltage is higher, in agreement with the above discussion (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0037_R_d19439662e1722" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nano Energy Adv. Funct. Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs6431-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c67acf62-5e9b-44f4-abae-ce950805213d/advs6431-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c67acf62-5e9b-44f4-abae-ce950805213d/advs6431-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5b8cf456-d70a-4879-95e3-4148e21e32a5/advs6431-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c67acf62-5e9b-44f4-abae-ce950805213d/advs6431-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs6431-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202304067" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The charge density difference map of the Na layer and transition metal layer in NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O structure. DOS of b) NaNMO and c) NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O. d) Schematic that illustrates NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O crystal structure with and without Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy. e) The Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy formation energy of NaNMO and NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The energy required to be absorbed when the intact crystal structure transforms into a structure containing Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy is known as the Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy formation energy. A lower Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy formation energy means that less energy needs to be absorbed for Na<sup>+</sup> extraction during charging. Therefore, we calculated and compared the Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy formation energy of four materials to better understand the influence of doping elements on Na<sup>+</sup> extraction. According to Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a> (Supporting Information), the Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy formation energy around K<sup>+</sup> (2.44&nbsp;eV) of NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMO is much lower than that of the pristine (2.99&nbsp;eV), implying that K<sup>+</sup> can enhance the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> in the bulk phase via lowering the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barrier in the low voltage region. The apparent decrease in Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy formation energy may be due to the strong K‒O bond and the more open Na layer spacing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0015_R_d19439662e1801" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Electrochim. Acta ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Nat. Commun. Electrochim. Acta">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is also observed that the Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy formation energy of NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O (2.50&nbsp;eV), although slightly higher than that of NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMO, is still significantly lower than that of the pristine (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs6431-fig-0005">5d,e</a>). Namely, the co-substituted NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O provides a faster Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channel than NaNMO, delivering favorable electrochemical performance in the voltage region of 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs6431-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs6431-sec-0080-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we have proposed a K/Ti co-substitution strategy to stabilize the O3-type Na<sub>0.99</sub>K<sub>0.01</sub>Ni<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode for high-energy and long-cycle NIBs. The obtained NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O shows a highly reversible O3-P3 phase transition during charge-discharge, and has an ultra-low volume change (0.59%) upon charging to 4.0&nbsp;V. Such enhancement is because that Ti<sup>4+</sup> can inhibit the spontaneous oxidation reactions and the release of lattice Na via promoting electron delocalization. The lower Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy formation energy (2.50 vs 2.99&nbsp;eV) in NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O than that in NaNMO facilitates the bulk Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. Hence, the modified NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O delivers impressive electrochemical properties including high reversible capacity (128&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), outstanding capacity retention (81% after 400 cycles for half cell, 73% after 500 cycles for full cell), and rate capability (97&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 5&nbsp;C). This multi-ion synergistic modification strategy compensates for the single effect and side effects associated with single-element doping and will open a new way to design advanced energy materials for electrochemical energy storage.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs6431-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs6431-sec-0090-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs6431-sec-0100">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs6431-sec-0110">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs6431-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs6431-sec-0120-title"> Cathode Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>The Na<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>K<i><sub>x</sub></i>Ni<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5-</sub><i><sub>y</sub></i>Ti<i><sub>y</sub></i>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.01; <i>y</i> = 0, 0.1) materials were synthesized by sol–gel method and labeled as NaNMO, NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMO, NaNMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O, NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O, respectively. A stoichiometric mixture of sodium acetate anhydrous (C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>Na, Aladdin, AR, 99.0%), potassium acetate (C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>3</sub>KO<sub>2</sub>, Aladdin, AR, 99.0%), nickel acetate tetrahydrate (C<sub>4</sub>H<sub>6</sub>NiO<sub>4</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O, Aladdin, AR, 99.0%), manganese acetate tetrahydrate (C<sub>4</sub>H<sub>6</sub>MnO<sub>4</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O, Aladdin, AR, 99.0%), and citric acid (C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>8</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, Macklin, AR, 99.5%) were dissolved in the distilled water and stirred well to form metal salt solution and citric acid solution, respectively (M: CA = 1:1, M = Na&nbsp;+&nbsp;Ni&nbsp;+&nbsp;Mn&nbsp;+&nbsp;K&nbsp;+&nbsp;Ti). Subsequently, stoichiometric amounts of tetra butyl titanate (C<sub>16</sub>H<sub>36</sub>O<sub>4</sub>Ti, Aladdin, AR, ≥99.0%) and the metal salt solution were slowly added to the stirring citric acid solution. Then, the ammonia solution was dropped to form a mixed solution with pH = 8, and the obtained solution was continuously stirred and heated at 80&nbsp;°C to form the gel. Finally, the gel was dried at 80&nbsp;°C to form precursors which were sintered at a high temperature to obtain cathode materials. The specific calcination was as follows: the precursors were calcined at 500&nbsp;°C for 5&nbsp;h in the air to remove the citric acid in precursors; calcined at 900&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h in the air; cooled to 700&nbsp;°C at a rate of 1&nbsp;°C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup>, and then quenched at room temperature. Additionally, to obtain air-exposed materials, NaNMO and NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O were exposed under the condition with a temperature of 25&nbsp;°C and a humidity of 55% for 1 day or 2 days. The materials after 50 cycles were labeled as C-NaNMO and C-NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O, respectively.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs6431-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs6431-sec-0130-title"> Cathode Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>To characterize the crystal structure of the synthesized materials, all the samples' X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns were observed on a Rigaku Ultima IV diffractometer with Cu K<i>α</i> radiation with the 2<i>θ</i> range of 10–80°. GSASII software was used to further Rietveld refinement of the XRD data as a means to investigate the lattice structure. The ex situ XRD test was performed on the electrodes at different voltages of charge/discharge obtained by dissembling the coin-type cell in an argon-filled glovebox. The in situ XRD analysis was performed using a homemade in situ cell equipped with thin aluminum windows. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM, JEOL JSM-7001F) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM, JEOL, JEM-2010F) were used to characterize the morphology and interlayer structure of the materials. The elemental distribution of the materials was identified by Energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) mapping. To detect the electronic state of the elements on the surface, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) data were gathered on an ESCALAB 250XI spectrometer. The surface functional groups and structural vibrational modes of the materials were characterized by Fourier Transform Infrared Spectrometer (FTIR, Thermo Scientific Nicolet iS20) and Raman (LabRam HR Evolution).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs6431-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs6431-sec-0140-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>To prepare the working electrodes, the cathode material, Super P, and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) were mixed well by grinding in a mass ratio of 8:1:1 and then dissolved in an appropriate amount of <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP). The obtained slurry was uniformly applied to the Al foil and quickly dried on an electric heating plate at 60&nbsp;°C. The obtained electrodes were further vacuum dried at 120&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h and then punched into round slices with a diameter of 12&nbsp;mm. The typical loading of active materials was 2.2&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. To evaluate the electrochemical capability, the coin-type (CR2025) half cell was assembled in an argon-filled glovebox (MBraun, Germany, H<sub>2</sub>O and O<sub>2</sub> &lt; 0.5&nbsp;ppm) with Na metal sheet as the anode, the synthetic electrodes as the cathode, glass fiber filter (Whatman) as the separator, and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in ethylene carbonate (EC) and propylene carbonate (PC) (1:1 in volume, 5% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC)) as the electrolyte. The coin-type full cell was assembled using NaK<sub>0.01</sub>NMTi<sub>0.1</sub>O as the cathode and hard carbon as the anode. The hard carbon (HC) electrodes were prepared in the way same as the above working electrode. The N/P value of the full cell was set to 1.05, and the specific capacity of the full cell was calculated based on the mass of the cathode active material. The Galvanostatic charge/discharge cycling tests were carried out on a LAND (Wuhan, China) test instrument at room temperature between 2.0 and 4.0&nbsp;V (1 C = 240&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>). Cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements were examined at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) tests were measured with a frequency range from 100000 to 0.01&nbsp;Hz and an amplitude of 5&nbsp;mV using an electrochemical workstation (CHI660D, Shanghai, Chenhua). For the Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) measurements, the coin-type cell was charged/discharged at 0.1 C for 4&nbsp;min and then executed a relaxation for 30&nbsp;min.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs6431-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs6431-sec-0150-title"> Computational Methods</h4>
                  
                  <p>All polarized density functional theory (DFT) calculations were performed with the Vienna Ab initio Simulation Package (VASP) in 96-atom supercell models of Na<sub>24</sub>Ni<sub>12</sub>Mn<sub>12</sub>O<sub>48</sub>, Na<sub>23</sub>KNi<sub>12</sub>Mn<sub>12</sub>O<sub>48</sub>, Na<sub>24</sub>Ni<sub>12</sub>Mn<sub>10</sub>Ti<sub>2</sub>O<sub>48</sub>, and Na<sub>23</sub>KNi<sub>12</sub>Mn<sub>10</sub>Ti<sub>2</sub>O<sub>48</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0038_R_d19439662e2066" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Phys. Rev. B, Condens. Matter">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof revised for solids functional (PBEsol) had been applied to the exchange-correlation function, using the generalized gradient approximation form (GGA-PBE).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0039_R_d19439662e2071" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The projector augmented wave (PAW) method was applied with the plane-wave energy cutoff of 520&nbsp;eV.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0040_R_d19439662e2077" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Phys. Rev. B">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> All the atomic coordinates and lattice parameters were fully relaxed by the conjugate gradient algorithm, where the convergence criteria of the energy and force acting on each atom were less than 10<sup>−5</sup>&nbsp;eV and 0.05&nbsp;eV Å<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The Monkhorst-Pack scheme 2&nbsp;×&nbsp;3&nbsp;×&nbsp;1 <i>k</i>-point mesh was used for relaxation. Due to the strongly correlated 3<i>d</i>-electrons, the Hubbard <i>U</i> values for Ni 3<i>d</i>, Mn 3<i>d</i>, and Ti 3<i>d</i> were 6.30, 4.95, and 2.5&nbsp;eV, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs6431-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs6431-bib-0041_R_d19439662e2099" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Phys. Rev. B">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>

         <div class="accordion article-accordion">
            <h2><a href="#" class="accordion__control" role="button" aria-controls="advs6431-bibl-0001" data-references="/action/ajaxShowEnhancedAbstract?widgetId=5cf4c79f-0ae9-4dc5-96ce-77f62de7ada9&amp;ajax=true&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202304067&amp;pbContext=%3Bjournal%3Ajournal%3A21983844%3Bpage%3Astring%3AArticle%2FChapter+View%3Bwebsite%3Awebsite%3Aadvanced%3Bctype%3Astring%3AJournal+Content%3BrequestedJournal%3Ajournal%3A21983844%3Bissue%3Aissue%3Adoi%5C%3A10.1002%2Fadvs.v10.32%3Barticle%3Aarticle%3Adoi%5C%3A10.1002%2Fadvs.202304067%3Bwgroup%3Astring%3APublication+Websites%3BpageGroup%3Astring%3APublication+Pages%3BsubPage%3Astring%3AFull+Text&amp;widgetKey=ux3-publicationContent-widget_5cf4c79f-0ae9-4dc5-96ce-77f62de7ada9_3067_en&amp;accordionHeadingWrapper=h2&amp;onlyLog=true" aria-expanded="false"><span class="section__title" id="references-section-1">References</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a></h2>
